    A New Class of Dengue and West Nile Virus Protease Inhibitors with Submicromolar Activity in Reporter Gene DENV-2 Protease and Viral Replication Assays

    Abstract

    Dengue and West Nile virus are rapidly spreading global pathogens for which no specific therapeutic treatments are available. One of the promising targets for drug discovery against dengue and other flaviviruses is the viral serine protease NS2B-NS3. We present the design, synthesis, and in vitro and cellular characterization of a novel chemotype of potent small-molecule non-peptidic dengue protease inhibitors derived from 4-benzyloxyphenylglycine. A newly developed luciferase-based DENV-2 protease reporter system in HeLa cells (DENV2proHeLa) was employed to determine the activity of the compounds in a cellular environment. Specificity and selectivity of the DENV2proHeLa system were confirmed by viral titer reduction assays. The compounds reach low micromolar to upper nanomolar inhibitory potency in cell-based assays, are selective against other serine proteases, and do not show relevant cytotoxicity. An extensive structure–activity relationship study provides a perspective for further drug development against flaviviral infections.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The global incidence of the mosquito-borne viral disease dengue has grown rapidly in the past years. A recent analysis estimates 390 million infections per year of which about one third are clinically recognized.(1) Dengue virus (DENV) infections can result in serious and life-threatening diseases like dengue fever, hemorrhagic fever, and dengue shock syndrome.(2,3) Despite an increasing impact of dengue fever, there are no specific therapeutic treatments available. Although a vaccine is approved in several countries, the vaccination of seronegative humans is not recommended.(4−6) Hence, there is an unmet medical need for antiviral agents against infections with DENV and other flaviviruses like West Nile and Zika virus.
    Similar to DENV, the mosquito-borne West Nile Virus (WNV) is a pathogen of global occurrence. Although most infections with WNV are asymptomatic, many patients develop flu-like symptoms or more severe diseases like encephalitis. Unfortunately, neither an effective antiviral therapy nor a vaccine is available.(7)
    As witnessed by the success of marketed drugs that target viral enzymes in hepatitis C virus (HCV)(8) and in human immunodeficiency virus (HIV),(9) viral proteases are attractive targets for the development of antiviral drugs.(10) The flaviviral protease complex (NS2B-NS3) in DENV, WNV, etc. is responsible for cleavage of the viral polyprotein into separate functional proteins and is therefore essential for virus replication.(10,11)
    The flaviviral proteases are serine proteases with a chymotrypsin-like fold and have a preference for substrates with dibasic motifs in P1 and P2.(11,12) Therefore, it is not surprising that most of the inhibitors developed so far contain highly charged recognition elements, frequently with a di- or polybasic motif. Combination of basic peptide sequences with an electrophilic group such as aldehyde or boronic acid, which are capable to form covalent bonds with the catalytic serine, results in high-affinity inhibitors.(12−14) The efficacy of such inhibitors in cellular assay systems is often limited by pharmacokinetic liabilities due to high molecular weight and pronounced polarity of the highly basic side chains (see Figure 1A). In most of the accomplished DENV and WNV protease inhibitor studies, cellular activities were not considered in the compound design. Structure–activity relationships were mainly focused on inhibition of isolated viral proteases. This approach has, to our knowledge, not yet delivered any DENV or WNV protease inhibitors with potential for clinical application. Whereas several classes of inhibitors reach IC50 values in the nanomolar range under biochemical assay conditions, the EC50 values from cell-based assays are often significantly higher. The biochemical and cellular assay data collected in our group over the past years indicate a lack of correlation between these readouts. In other words, while the biochemical assay is highly useful to efficiently screen a large number of compounds and analyze structure-activity relationships on the molecular target interaction level, its utility to predict antiviral activity is limited. To address this shortcoming, we developed an HTS-capable intracellular DENV-2 protease screening assay based on a luciferase reporter system (DENV2proHeLa) that allows the determination of protease activity in a cellular context and integrates the simultaneous assessment of membrane permeability, metabolic stability, and cytotoxicity. Furthermore, the protease in DENV2proHeLa cells is capable of interacting with human host proteins and the ER membrane and can therefore be studied in a more biologically meaningful environment as compared to an isolated protease in a biochemical assay. For a selection of previously reported compounds from our group, we found a good correlation between data generated in the DENV2proHeLa and virus titer reduction assays.
    Figure 1B depicts the structure of the newly discovered compound class. Compounds in this series contain only one basic residue and have a much smaller molecular mass than most previously reported flaviviral protease inhibitors of similar or higher potency. These factors are expected to lead to increased bioavailability and higher activity in cellular assays.(17,18) In contrast to the peptide-boronic acid inhibitors reported before,(14) which have high target affinities but also interact strongly with off-targets such as thrombin, the new class of compounds do not incorporate an electrophilic element for covalent binding to the catalytic serine. Inherited from previous work is a phenylglycine (Phg) or 4-benzyloxyphenylglycine element.(15,19,20) However, in the current compound class, this amino acid is coupled to lipophilic alkyl chains or ring systems via an amide bond and not to other peptidic elements as described before. The phenylglycine carboxylic acid moiety is connected via a second amide bond to a phenyl group that is preferably further substituted with basic residues. We herein present the synthesis, in vitro and cellular structure–activity relationships, off-target selectivity, and stability evaluation of this novel compound class.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Chemistry

    All final compounds and intermediates were obtained by solid-phase peptide synthesis according to the Fmoc protocol. The 4-benzyloxyphenylglycine derivatives (59–67) were obtained by O-alkylation of the corresponding 4-hydroxyphenylglycine as previously described.(15,21) Phenylglycine derivatives were N-Fmoc protected (compounds 68–76) as described by Dener et al.(22) Peptide coupling reactions were performed with COMU/TMP (on resin) or HATU / TMP (in solution) since it was shown that under those conditions, the α-carbon racemization of the phenylglycine derivative can be minimized to a negligible extent.(23) As further detailed below, stereochemical purity was verified for the compounds presented here.
    Scheme 1 depicts the synthetic approach for the on-resin-synthesis of compounds and their guanidinylation reactions. 6-Aminonicotinonitrile was reduced to the respective amine 77 using palladium on carbon under hydrogen atmosphere and selectively N-Fmoc protected using Fmoc-OSu under nitrogen atmosphere. The aniline nitrogen of the N-Fmoc protected 4-(aminomethyl)aniline or 5-(aminomethyl)pyridin-2-amine was attached to chlorotrityl chloride resin under basic conditions. After Fmoc deprotection, the respective compound was obtained by solid-phase peptide synthesis according to the Fmoc strategy. After cleavage from the resin with 5% TFA in DCM, the resulting aniline was converted to the respective di-Boc-guanidine using N,N′-di-Boc-1H-pyrazole-1-carboxamidine, which is known for efficient guanidinylation reactions in peptide synthesis.(24) This procedure remained unsuccessful for the 2-aminopyridine 12, which was transformed into the respective guanidine moiety by treatment with N,N′-di-Boc-S-methylisothiourea and mercuric chloride in THF, as described before.(25) The Boc-protected guanidine moiety was deprotected using 50% TFA in DCM. All other compounds with guanidine groups were synthesized under similar conditions. The aniline compound 11 was reacted with sodium cyanate and acetic acid to afford the urea analog 24.
    The synthesis of 9 and 18 is provided in Scheme 2. 4-(Aminomethyl)benzonitrile (56) was synthesized from 4-cyanobenzylbromide, which was substituted to the azide and directly reduced to the respective amine with tin(II)chloride dihydrate in 94% overall yield. The benzamidine-containing compound 18 was obtained via the respective nitrile 9: first, (S)-2-(4-(benzyloxy)phenyl)-2-hexanamidoacetic acid (57) was synthesized on chlorotrityl chloride resin and coupled to 4-(aminomethyl)benzonitrile with HATU/TMP in solution. The resulting compound 9 was treated with hydroxylamine to achieve the amidoxime intermediate, which was directly reduced using tin(II)chloride to the respective benzamidine 18. Syntheses of caps 80–83 and other intermediates are described in the Supplementary Information.

    Structure–Activity Relationships

    Four series of compounds were evaluated in biochemical enzymatic assays against DENV-2 and WNV proteases. Aprotinin and compounds MB-8 and MB-53 were used as inhibitor controls (Table 1). All three compounds are known inhibitors of flaviviral proteases with high potencies in biochemical assays.(15,19,26) Thrombin and trypsin were evaluated as potential off-targets. All compounds with low cytotoxicity (>80% signal compared to nontreated control) at the screening concentration were further evaluated in the DENV2proHeLa assay. The SAR exploration was focused on basic C-terminal moieties, different substitutions of the benzyl residue, and variation of lipophilic N-terminal caps. From a medicinal chemistry perspective, our strategy—the creation of compound libraries by building block assembly and derivatization—provides a fast track to diverse compounds, which are not easily accessible otherwise, considering for example the use of the racemization-prone phenylglycine in synthetic procedures. While this approach is inspired from amino acid assembly for peptides, it can be effectively applied on other chemical building blocks for lead optimization.
    Table 1. Inhibitory Activity of Compounds with Different C-Terminal Residues against Isolated DENV and WNV Proteases, in the Cellular DENV Protease Reporter Assay (DENV2proHeLa) and Cytotoxicity in HeLa Cells
    a

    IC50 values against DENV NS2B-NS3 serotype 2 at 50 μM substrate concentration.

    b

    IC50 values against WNV NS2B-NS3 at 50 μM substrate concentration.

    c

    EC50 values against DENV NS2B-NS3 serotype 2 in HeLa cells.

    d

    Measured in HeLa cells.

    e

    Measurements with aprotinin were performed with a concentration of 30 nM for DENV-2 and WNV proteases. If inhibition ≤10% = no inhibition (n.i.). n.d. = not determined. All measurements were carried out in triplicate.

    Intracellular DENV Protease Reporter Gene System (DENV2proHeLa)

    In order to study inhibitory activity in a target-specific, intracellular high-throughput format, we developed a DENV serotype 2 protease reporter assay in HeLa cells. This reporter system was recently used to evaluate prodrug activation by the DENV protease.(27)
    In order to preserve the natural conformation and interaction of NS2B and NS3pro, NS2B and NS3 are present as full-length proteins in the DENV2proHeLa system. The Renilla reniformis luciferase was used as the reporter enzyme. The N-terminus of the luciferase is connected via a dengue protease cleavage site to the oxygen-dependent degradation domain (ODD) of HIF1α (Figure 2A). The ODD domain initiates rapid proteolytic degradation of the fused protein by ubiquitination of PEST sequences within this domain.(28−32) The linkage of a luciferase reporter to an HIF-1α ODD degradation domain, its degradation by the proteasome, and the use for compound screenings in cells and in vivo were shown before.(33−37) The natural recognition sites NS2A/NS2B, NS3/NS4A, and NS4B/NS5 of the flaviviral polyprotein were explored as cleavage sites for the linker.(38) When co-transfected with a mammalian expression plasmid coding for the DENV protease, incorporation of the NS2A/NS2B cleavage site in the linker region resulted in the most significant increase in luciferase activity, as compared to other polyprotein cleavage sites (Figure S1). This increase was not observed when a DENV protease construct without catalytic competence (S135A mutant) was co-transfected. Recently, sensors of cellular flavivirus NS2B-NS3 cleavage activity have been generated by linking GFP-based reporters with protease cleavage sites to a quenching peptide. Those sensors reported a fluorescence increase in stably transduced cells upon DENV-2 infection.(39)
    The stable co-transfection of the DENV-2 NS2B/NS3 protease and the ODD-luciferase reporter construct resulted in a cell clone (DENV2proHeLa) with satisfying characteristics and wild-type morphology. Integration of the two sequences was achieved without destruction of relevant genes or regulatory sequences within the genome, and sequencing of the amplified inserts proved the integrity of both constructs. Several published inhibitors with known antiviral activity were used to further explore the characteristics of the system and its utility for compound screening (Table S2).
    Treatment of DENV2proHeLa cells with well-characterized inhibitors of the DENV protease had a pronounced effect on the luciferase signal, already after 24 h of treatment. EC50 values determined in the DENV2proHeLa system were highly reproducible, operator-independent, and correlated well with previous data from virus titer reduction assays (Figure 2B,C). The statistical parameters Z′ factor and signal window were calculated for four independent experiments (see Figure S2 for further details). The Z′ factor was above 0.5 for all experiments, indicating good applicability for high-throughput screening. EC50 values, exemplary dose–response curves, and structures of published covalent and noncovalent inhibitors that were used for the correlation are provided in the Supplementary Information. Hence, we conclude that DENV2proHeLa is a valuable system to evaluate compounds targeted against the DENV protease in a high-throughput format before proceeding to a virus titer reduction assay.

    Variation of the C-Terminal Residue

    Basic residues were primarily evaluated at this position (see Table 1). The lysine and alkylamine analogs 1–4 showed no or minor inhibition in the biochemical assays, similar to nonbasic or weakly basic compounds (e.g., 6, 7, 9, 11). The inhibition increased with higher basicity of the residues. Especially phenylalkylamines or phenylguanidines led to potent inhibitors. Ki values of 3.8 μM for 14 and 9.8 μM for 20 at DENV-2 protease were determined using Cheng–Prusoff plots (see Supplementary Information). Compounds 24 and 25 can form similar hydrogen bonds with the protease and were designed as nonbasic guanidine replacements. However, a basic character is required at this position since a significant decrease of inhibition in all assays was measured. Surprisingly, the amidine-containing compound 18 had weak activity. Meta-substituted derivatives had lower potency compared to their para-substituted derivatives (e.g., 15, 23). A methylene group between the amide nitrogen and the phenyl ring was needed for optimal activity (19 vs 20). Most compounds had similar activities in the biochemical and the cellular protease assays, but compounds 13, 14, and 19 could not be measured due to cytotoxicity at the screening concentration. Some of the compounds had higher activity in the cellular environment than in an isolated protease assay, an observation that was made before by other groups and was regarded as a consequence of the artificial composition of the biochemical assay,(40−43) which is carried out at basic pH in the presence of detergents and polyols.(44) Other hypothetical explanations for increased cellular activity can be an intracellular accumulation or the requirement of a nearly complete processing of the viral polyprotein for viral infectivity.(40) Noteworthy is the upper-nanomolar DENV2proHeLa EC50 of compound 20. The N,N-dimethylamine derivative 16 showed no inhibition in the biochemical assays but a high inhibition in the cell-based assay, which could be due to intracellular demethylation to yield the monomethylamine derivative of compound 14. The latter compound was a potent inhibitor in the biochemical assays, but could not be evaluated in cells due to its low CC50 of 4.8 μM. Various heterocycles were evaluated: The 1H-indazole-containing compound 8 had an EC50 of around 7 μM. The pyridine-containing compounds 7 and 12 inhibited 50% of the protease at 12.5 μM in cells, whereas 1-(pyridin-2-yl)guanidine-containing 21 had an EC50 of 6 μM.

    Variation of the Benzyloxyphenylglycine Moiety

    Table 2 presents combinations of the C-terminal 4-phenylguanidine with variable benzyloxyphenylglycine moieties. The benzyloxy residue is essential for activity. The phenylglycine-containing compound 26 was inactive in all assays. Furthermore, the unsubstituted d-derivative (27) had a 20-fold lower inhibition compared to the l-isomer (20). This is in strong contrast to previously published inhibitor structures, which showed about 3-fold higher activity for d-derivatives(15) and may be seen as an indication for a fundamentally different binding mode of the present new chemotype. The electron-withdrawing halogen substituent containing compounds in l- and d-configurations had a 2- to 5-fold higher inhibitory potency in biochemical assays (28, 29, 32, 33). However, substitutions at the benzyloxy residue were not well tolerated in the DENV2proHeLa assay. Other substituents like 3-methoxy, 4-cyano, or 4-tert-butyl decreased the activity of the compounds in all assays.
    Table 2. Inhibitory Activity of Compounds with Variations in the Benzyloxyphenylglycine Moiety against Isolated DENV and WNV Protease, in the Cellular DENV Protease Reporter Assay (DENV2proHeLa) and Cytotoxicity in HeLa Cells
    a

    IC50 values against DENV NS2B-NS3 serotype 2 at 50 μM substrate concentration.

    b

    IC50 values against WNV NS2B-NS3 at 50 μM substrate concentration.

    c

    EC50 values against DENV NS2B-NS3 serotype 2 in HeLa cells.

    d

    Measured in HeLa cells. If inhibition ≤10% = no inhibition (n.i.). n.d. = not determined. All measurements were carried out in triplicate.

    Variation of the N-Terminal Cap

    Mostly, lipophilic alkyl and phenyl residues were evaluated as N-terminal caps. The length of the alkyl chains had a major influence on the activity, with clear dependencies between lipophilicity and activities in the biochemical and cellular assays. The minor inhibition by the acetyl analog in the cell-based assay could be increased by extending the chain length. The hexanoic acid cap (20) showed a nearly 6-fold higher activity compared to the valeric acid cap (36). The heptanoic and octanoic acid caps gave comparable EC50 values of 0.52 and 0.83 μM, respectively. Those compounds are therefore novel promising structures with high activity in a cellular environment without relevant cytotoxicity. The positive effect of chain length on potency tapered off in the C10 region and at more complex (cyclo-) aliphatic caps. A benzoic acid cap led to low inhibition, whereas cyclohexyloxy-, phenyloxy-, and benzyloxybenzoic acid derivatives showed a 2- or 3-fold higher inhibition in the biochemical assays compared to compound 20. For these inhibitors, comparable EC50 values were measured in the cellular protease assay. With a 4-chloro substituent (46), a nearly 5-fold higher inhibition in biochemical assays was achieved, whereas the 3-methoxy substituent (47) led to comparable inhibition to the unsubstituted derivative (45). Similar values were observed in the cellular protease assay for those derivatives except for 46, which was significantly less potent.
    An up to 9-fold higher activity was observed with the biphenyl-4-carboxylic acid and 2-(biphenyl-4-yl)acetic acid caps in the DENV biochemical assay, and an up to 7-fold higher activity was observed in the WNV assay. Note that 2-(biphenyl-4-yl)acetic acid (INN: felbinac) is a clinically used cyclooxygenase inhibitor.(45) The incorporation of biphenyl-2-carboxylic acid as a cap led to 2-fold lower inhibition compared to that of compound 20. Most improvements of the biphenyl caps could not be followed up in the DENV2proHeLa assay, where the long alkyl chain cap-containing compounds achieved the highest activities (e.g., 20). Especially compound 52 showed a big deviation between the biochemical vs cellular protease assays: The analog with a 2-(biphenyl-4-yl)acetic acid (= felbinac) cap had the highest in vitro activity but reached only an EC50 of 20 μM in the DENV2proHeLa system. However, future work may be aimed at the exploration of the wide diversity of clinically used arylacetic and arylpropionic acids as N-terminal capping moieties. Some of the caps resulted in cytotoxicity at 50 μM (42, 43, 45, 46, 47), but at the DENV2proHeLa screening concentration (12.5 μM), no cytotoxicity was observed.

    Fragment Merging

    Selected fragments of the compounds described in Tables 2–3 were merged, thus combining the most active residues in the biochemical assays (see Table 4). Compound 53 showed promising inhibition in biochemical assays but could not be further evaluated due to its cytotoxicity. Compounds 54 and 55 are combinations of the most active fragments in the biochemical assays. The combination of 2,6-dichloro-(4-benzyloxy)-phenylglycine, 2-(biphenyl-4-yl)acetic acid, and the phenylguanidine moiety afforded compound 55 with upper nanomolar potencies in biochemical assays. Ki values were measured in DENV and WNV biochemical assays and were found to be 0.46 and 1.0 μM, respectively. In spite of the promising potency in in vitro assays, compound 55 had significantly lower activity (EC50: 39 μM) in the DENV2proHeLa assay. The same observation was made for compound 54. The high molecular masses of these compounds (683 and 667 g/mol) may result in an inferior pharmacokinetic profile and low membrane permeability. Compounds 32 and 52, which contain the same structural moieties as compound 55, were also significantly less potent in the cell-based assay in comparison to compound 20.

    Membrane Permeability

    The passive membrane permeabilities of compounds 20, 32, 37, 38, 39, 42, 43, 44, 45, 47, 52, 54, and 55 and a set of drug references were evaluated using the precoated trilayer parallel artificial membrane permeability assay (PAMPA).(46,47) The permeability of the reference compounds was in accordance with literature values,(46) but compounds 32, 39, 45, 47, 52, 54, and 55 appeared to be nonpermeable (see Table 5). This offers an explanation for the lower activity of compounds 32, 52, 54, and 55 in the DENV2proHeLa system compared to their inhibition in the biochemical DENV assay, although an active transport or endocytotic uptake of these basic compounds is possible, especially in hepatocytes such as the Huh-7 cell line used for the viral replication experiments. Compounds 37, 38, 42, 43, and 44, which had high activities in the DENV2proHeLa system, are somewhat permeable but significantly less than the tested standards caffeine, carbamazepine, and phenytoin. The most active inhibitor in the DENV2proHeLa assay (compound 20) showed significantly higher membrane permeability than the other compounds. Notably, the molecular masses of the cell-active compounds 20 (502 g/mol), 37 (516 g/mol), and 38 (530 g/mol) are very close to the Lipinski criteria,(17,48) whereas compounds 32, 39, 42, 43, 44, 45, 47, 52, 54, and 55 violate this rule.

    Selectivity and Binding Mode

    To determine the selectivity of the inhibitors, biochemical assays with the serine proteases thrombin and trypsin were performed (see the Supporting Information). None of the compounds showed significant inhibition of thrombin at 25 μM, which stands in marked contrast to some of the flaviviral protease inhibitor series before, in particular the peptide boronic acids.(14,49) Three of the compounds were moderately active against trypsin in a screen (above 50% inhibition at 50 μM). The IC50 of compounds 18, 27, and 48 against trypsin were determined to be 45, 44, and 31 μM, respectively. All other compounds had low off-target activities.
    Cheng–Prusoff plots of several inhibitors in biochemical assays with DENV and WNV proteases indicate a competitive inhibition mechanisms for this inhibitor series (see the Supporting Information). Furthermore, a tryptophan quenching assay(50) was used to study the binding mode of a selected compound at DENV-2 protease. For that purpose,4 compound 51 was designed because 2,2′-bithiophene-5-carboxylic acid quenches tryptophan fluorescence effectively.(15) Compound 51 had an IC50 of approximately 6 μM against DENV and WNV in in vitro assays. The results of the fluorescence quenching experiments indicate a binding of the inhibitor in the active site of the enzyme. As shown in Figure 3, the addition of compound 51 to DENV-2 protease results in concentration-dependent quenching of the fluorescence of tryptophan residues of the protease. Aprotinin is a known competitive inhibitor for DENV protease and was also co-crystallized with DENV-3 protease.(26) Aprotinin displaces compound 51 from the DENV-2 protease, providing further evidence for specific binding at the active site (see Figure 3).
    Compound 51 can be used as a quencher for the protease autofluorescence, particularly in displacement assays for compounds that lack moieties capable of interacting with tryptophan fluorescence.(15) In comparison to commercially available inhibitors for the flaviviral protease (e.g., aprotinin, ARDP0006), the inhibitor offers an advantage through its high potency in both biochemical and cellular assays.(43)

    Enantiomeric Purity of Compounds

    The acidity of the α-proton in phenylglycine results in a relatively high racemization tendency of this building block.(23,51,52) Stereochemical purity and stability of the compounds were assessed by chiral HPLC. Exemplary compounds were analyzed, and enantiomeric purity was above 95% in all cases (see the Supplementary Information for more details). Additionally, compound 20 was tested for its racemization tendency in assay buffers used in this study. Racemization was negligible after incubation for 96 h at 37 °C in phosphate-buffered saline (pH 7.4), biochemical DENV assay buffer (pH 9.0), and DENV2proHeLa medium (pH 7.0). A highly basic sodium hydroxide solution (pH 13) and an incubation of 96 h were required for full racemization of the tested compound (see the Supplementary Information).

    Docking Studies

    Docking studies were performed using GOLD 5.1(53) and the X-ray structure of DENV-3 protease with a resolution of 2.3 Å reported by Noble et al. (PDB: 3U1I).(26) The crystal structure of the DENV-3 protease in complex with a tetrapeptidyl aldehyde was removed from the dimer structure. All water molecules, sulfate ions, and the covalently bound peptidyl aldehyde inhibitor were removed. The structure by Noble et al. was selected because no X-ray structure with an inhibitor bound to the closed form of DENV-2 protease is available and serotypes 2 and 3 have a high degree of conservation of the active sites. Docking studies were focused on compounds 20 and 43. Compound 20 was chosen for docking studies because of its high activity in the cellular DENV2proHeLa assay. Inhibitor 43 showed high potencies in biochemical and cellular assays and contains a 4-phenoxybenzoic acid cap. The docking studies were done mainly to evaluate whether both the flexible hexanoic acid cap and the large, relatively rigid 4-phenoxybenzoic acid cap show a tendency to bind in the same binding area in the active site of the enzyme, and the simulations support this notion (see Figure 4). The positively charged phenylguanidine moiety is placed in the S1 pocket, where it forms an ionic interaction with the negatively charged aspartate residue on the bottom of the pocket. It is known that basic residues are preferred in this pocket.(11) (4-Benzyloxy)-l-phenylglycine is located in the S2 pocket. The phenyl ring of the phenylglycine is in close contact with the catalytic histidine with a possibility to form Π–Π-stacking interactions. Phenylglycine and its derivatives were already described as preferred moieties in HCV protease inhibitors at this position.(54,55) The hexanoic acid (compound 20) and the 4-phenoxybenzoic acid cap (compound 43) were placed in the S3 and S4 pockets where mainly lipophilic interactions occur.

    Antiviral Activity in Virus Titer Reduction Assay

    The compounds with the highest potency in the DENV2proHeLa assay were further evaluated in a virus titer reduction assay using DENV-2-infected human hepatic carcinoma cells (Huh-7). The previously reported DENV-2 inhibitor MB-53(15) was used as the reference compound. No cytotoxic effects were observed at the chosen concentration ranges. All tested compounds showed reduction of viral titers at a concentration of 12.5 μM (see Figure 5). Whereas the pyridin-2-yl guanidine derivative 21 and the 4-benzyloxybenzoic acid derivatives 45 and 47 showed moderate activity, treatment with compounds 20, 22, 37, 43, and 49 reduced viral titers to less than 25% of the control. In addition, antiviral dose–response curves were obtained for three compounds: 20, 38, and 43 reduced viral titers at least by 50% at 12.5 μM (see Figure 5) and had EC50 values between 0.49 and 3 μM in DENV2proHeLa. When comparing the cellular data of compounds 20 and compounds 36–39, which differ only in the length of their alkyl chain, maximum potency was obtained for a C6 chain with a drop in potencies for longer alkyl chains. In accordance with inhibition in DENV2proHeLa, all three compounds reduced the virus titer with EC50 values in the low micromolar range. Compound 20 had the most promising EC50 of 0.89 μM. In contrast, compounds 38 and 43 had EC50 values of 7.2 and 9.3 μM, respectively (Figure 6).

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    We here describe the design, synthesis, and testing of small-molecule DENV protease inhibitors with drug-like properties. Several derivatives have nanomolar activity in a cellular DENV-2 protease reporter assay (DENV2proHeLa) and stable stereochemistry under physiological and assay conditions. The high activity of selected compounds was further confirmed in viral titer reduction assays. Compound 20, with proven passive membrane permeability, had submicromolar activity in both cellular assays. Most of the analogs presented here are nontoxic up to 50 μM and have low off-target activity. Future efforts in medicinal chemistry will aim at the optimization of cellular activity of the compound class presented here, such as the development of a prodrug strategy for the guanidinophenyl residue.
    In contrast to, for example, boronic acids or some other classes of flaviviral protease inhibitors, the synthetic access to the analogs presented here is relatively facile. This favors the exploration of additional derivatives and makes them readily available. Besides their use as lead compounds for further drug discovery, these compounds can serve as reference inhibitors in both biochemical and cellular DENV-2 protease assay systems. Viral replication assays are usually performed under variable conditions (e.g., MOI, cell type, incubation time, medium composition) by different laboratories,(57) impeding the comparison of results, antiviral activity, and assay conditions (e.g., pH, presence and type of detergent or other additives). Considering this background, reference compounds with good synthetic accessibility provide an opportunity to calibrate results from various laboratories and assay setups against a common standard.
    In vitro target affinity is frequently difficult to translate into systems of higher complexity. In a cellular environment, pharmacokinetic effects determine the concentration at the site of target binding and the target is under the influence of biophysical factors that are difficult or impossible to reproduce under biochemical assay conditions, like molecular crowding, redox state, membrane binding, or formation of higher-order assemblies and structures. Therefore, it is attractive to employ cellular reporter gene assays, such as the DENV2proHeLa system, already at the early stages of compound characterization. The DENV2proHeLa incorporates pharmacokinetic effects and allows the evaluation of target engagement under physiological conditions, which includes the association of viral target(s) with organelles and (macro-) molecules of the host cell. In comparison to biochemical assays, the DENV2proHeLa results have an increased correlation to authentic virus titer reduction, which is considered the in vitro gold standard in antiviral drug discovery. The present study profited considerably from the ODD-↓-luciferase reporter gene protease assay, which was capable of identifying the nanomolar activity of the most promising lead structures in cells. The DENV2proHeLa assay also detected compounds with cellular activity that showed insignificant inhibition against the isolated DENV-2 protease: This indicates that factors beyond pharmacokinetics (such as folding of the target protein, interaction with other cellular components, etc.) influence the expression of cellular activity. The DENV2proHeLa assay can be performed in a high-throughput format and is significantly less time-consuming than virus titer reduction assays. We therefore consider it to be a useful bridge between the in vitro biochemical and viral replication assays. The presented paradigm can also be applied to the establishment of analogous assays for other intracellular proteases, such as the Corona virus protease, which is currently pursued in our group.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Reagents and Solvents

    All chemicals for the synthesis of precursors were obtained from Sigma-Aldrich (Germany), Alfa Aesar (Germany), Thermo Fisher Scientific (Germany), Acros Organics (Belgium), Thermo Fisher Scientific (United States), and TCI Europe (Belgium) and were of analytical grade. The amino acids were purchased from Carbolution Chemicals (Germany), Alfa Aesar (Germany), and TCI Europe (Belgium) or synthesized according to procedures described. COMU, HATU, and 2-chlorotrityl chloride resin (200–400 mesh); loading capacity: 1.6 mmol Cl–/g resin; cross-linked with 1% DVB was purchased from Carbolution Chemicals (Germany). All chemicals and solvents were purchased from commercial suppliers and were used without further purification.

    Equipment and Analytical Methods

    The progress of the reactions was determined by thin-layer chromatography (TLC) on Merck silica gel plates 60F254 (UV detection). Flash chromatography was performed on a Biotage Isolera One purification system using silica gel (0.060–0.200 mm) cartridges (KP-Sil) with UV detection at 254 and 280 nm. NMR spectra were recorded on a Varian NMR instrument at 300 or 500 MHz, 300 K; chemical shifts (δ) are given in parts per million (ppm). Residual peaks of nondeuterated solvents were used as the internal standard: CDCl3 (δ ppm: 7.26), D2O (δ ppm: 4.79), CD3OD (δ ppm: 3.31), DMSO-d6 (δ ppm: 2.50), CD3CN (δ ppm: 1.94), and acetone-d6 (δ ppm: 2.05). Coupling constants (J) are given in hertz (Hz). Multiplicity is reported as s (singlet), d (doublet), t (triplet), q (quartet), p (pentet), sext (sextet), dd (doublet of doublet), m (multiplet), and br (broad). Mass spectra (HR-ESI) of all compounds were measured on a Bruker micrOTOF-Q II instrument. Analysis of the compounds and intermediates was carried out using solutions in water, methanol, acetonitrile, or water/methanol (1:1). Melting points of selected compounds were determined with a Büchi M-560 melting point apparatus and are uncorrected.

    General Procedure for Peptide Coupling in Solution

    The respective carboxylic acid (1.0 equiv) and HATU (1.2 equiv) were suspended in DCM (2–5 mL). The mixture was cooled to 0 °C, and the respective amine (1.2 equiv) was added prior to the dropwise addition of TMP (1.3–3.2 equiv). DMF was added until all solids were dissolved, and the mixture was stirred at room temperature for 2 h. The reaction was quenched with the addition of a solution of TFA in DCM (5%, 5 mL). All solvents were removed in vacuo, and the resulting residue was dissolved in ethyl acetate. The ethyl acetate phase was washed twice with 1 N HCl, 0.05 N NaOH, and water. The combined organic phases were dried over anhydrous MgSO4, concentrated under reduced pressure, and purified by preparative HPLC. After purification, all organic solvents were evaporated and the compounds were freeze-dried in water.

    General Procedure for Guanidinylation of Amines

    The respective amine (1.0 equiv), N,N′-di-Boc-1H-pyrazole-1-carboxamidine (1.5 equiv), DMAP (0.3 equiv), and DIPEA (1.0 equiv) were dissolved or suspended in MeOH (5–20 mL). The mixture was allowed to stir for 2 or 3 days at room temperature. The solvent was removed in vacuo, and the resulting residue was dissolved in ethyl acetate or DCM. The organic phase was washed with 0.1 N HCl, 1 N NaOH, and water. The combined organic phase was dried over anhydrous MgSO4 and concentrated under reduced pressure. The resulting solid or oil was dissolved in TFA/DCM (1:1, 5–10 mL) and was stirred for 2–3 h. The mixture was concentrated and further co-evaporated with several portions of toluene. The residue was purified by preparative HPLC and freeze-dried in water.

    General Procedure for the Synthesis of Inhibitors and Intermediates on the Solid Support

    All peptides, peptide hybrid sequences, and intermediates (e.g., (S)-2-(4-(benzyloxy)phenyl)-2-hexanamidoacetic acid (57), (S)-4-((2-(4-(benzyloxy)phenyl)-2-hexanamidoacetamido)methyl)benzoic acid (58)) were assembled by stepwise solid-phase synthesis on CTC resin using the standard Fmoc-strategy, as previously described.(58,59) Solid-phase synthesis was done manually in plastic syringes equipped with a frit; all steps were performed at room temperature under continuous shaking. The first amino acid or respective building block (1.5–3.0 equiv) was added to the resin in a syringe and DMF (1 mL), and TMP (1.5–3.0 equiv) was added. The resin was shaken overnight and capped before the Fmoc deprotection step. Therefore, the resin was washed with 3× DMF, 3× DCM, and again 3× DMF and a capping solution (DCM/MeOH/TMP 80:15:5 (v/v), 1 mL per 100 mg resin) was added two times for 20 min. For Fmoc deprotection, a piperidine solution (10–20% in DMF) was added two times for 10 and 5 min. Following each deprotection or coupling step, the resin was washed with 3× DMF, 3× DCM, and again 3× DMF. COMU/TMP was used for all coupling steps. In detail, the coupling solution contained the Nα-Fmoc-protected amino acid or N-terminal carboxylic acid capping group (3.0 equiv), COMU (3.0 equiv), and TMP (3.9 equiv) in DMF (∼1.0 mL per 100 mg of resin). The solution was added to the resin, and the reaction was shaken for 60–120 min. Afterward, the resin was washed as described before. Fmoc deprotection and coupling steps were iteratively repeated until the desired sequence was obtained. The resin loaded with the finished compound or intermediate was washed five times with diethyl ether and dried under reduced pressure. The final product was cleaved off the resin three times with TFA/DCM solution (5:95, 1–2 mL per 100 mg resin), and the mixture was shaken for at least 30 min. The cleavage solution was dispensed into a round-bottom flask, dried under reduced pressure, and co-evaporated with toluene several times. All crude compounds were purified by preparative HPLC on an ÄKTA Purifier, GE Healthcare (Germany), with an RP-18 pre and main column (Rephospher, Dr. Maisch GmbH, Germany, C18-DE, 5 μm, 30 mm × 16 mm and 120 mm × 16 mm). The following conditions were used: eluent A, water (0.1% TFA); eluent B, methanol (0.1% TFA); flow rate, 8 mL/min; and gradient, 10% B (2.5 min), 100% B (23.5 min), 100% B (26 min), 10% B (26.1 min), and 10% B (30 min). Detection was performed at 214, 254, and 280 nm. After purification, methanol was evaporated, and the compounds and intermediates were freeze-dried in water and stored at −20 °C.

    DENV and WNV Protease Relative Inhibition Assay

    The DENV and WNV protease relative inhibition assays were performed as described before.(58,59) Continuous enzymatic assays were performed in black 96-well V-bottom plates (Greiner Bio-One, Germany) using a BMG Labtech Fluostar OPTIMA microtiter fluorescence plate reader at an excitation wavelength of 320 nm and a monitored emission wavelength of 405 nm. Stock solutions of the inhibitors (10 mM in DMSO) were diluted to a final concentration of 50 μM in triplicate and preincubated for 15 min with the DENV protease (100 nM) or WNV protease (150 nM) in the assay buffer (50 mM Tris–HCl pH 9, ethylene glycol (10% v/v), and 0.0016% Brij 58). The reaction was then initiated by the addition of the FRET substrate (final concentration 50 μM) to obtain a final assay volume of 100 μL per well. The enzymatic activity was monitored for 15 min and determined as a slope of relative fluorescence units per second (RFU/s) for each concentration. Percentage inhibition was calculated relative to a positive control (without the inhibitor), as a mean of the triplicates and respective standard deviation.

    DENV and WNV Protease Assays for IC50 Determination

    IC50 for DENV and WNV inhibitors were performed as described previously.(58,59) Continuous enzymatic assays were performed in black 96-well V-bottom plates (Greiner Bio-One, Germany) using a BMG Labtech Fluostar OPTIMA Microtiter fluorescence plate reader at an excitation wavelength of 320 nm and a monitored emission wavelength of 405 nm. Eight inhibitor concentrations covering the range 0–3, 0–6, 0–10, 0–15, 0–30, or 0–50 μM were chosen for analysis. Dilutions were made starting from 10 mM stock solutions in DMSO, and final concentrations were measured in triplicate. The inhibitors were preincubated for 15 min with the DENV protease (100 nM) or WNV protease (150 nM) in the assay buffer (50 mM Tris–HCl pH 9.0, ethylene glycol (10% v/v), and 0.0016% Brij 58). The enzymatic reaction was then initiated by the addition of the FRET substrate (final concentration 50 μM) to obtain a final assay volume of 100 μL per well. The enzymatic activity was monitored for 15 min and determined as a slope of relative fluorescence units per second (RFU/s) for each concentration. The mean and the standard deviation of the triplicates plotted against the corresponding concentration were used to determine the IC50 values on Prism 6.01 (Graphpad Software, Inc.) using nonlinear dose–response curves with variable slopes.

    Tryptophan Fluorescence Quenching Assay

    The tryptophan fluorescence quenching assay was performed as described by Bodenreider et al.(50) DENV protease (0.2 μM) was titrated with different amounts (0, 1.25, 2.5, 5, 10, 20, 30, 40, 50 μM) of inhibitor in assay buffer (50 mM Tris–HCl pH 9.0, ethylene glycol (10% v/v), and 0.0016% Brij 58), and the samples were incubated for 1 h at room temperature. For the fluorescence displacement experiment, the protease and NATA controls were incubated with the inhibitor (50 μM) and aprotinin (10 μM) under the same conditions. Fluorescence of the enzymes (0.2 μM DENV) in the presence of aprotinin (10 μM), without the inhibitor, was also determined and used to correct the results of aprotinin displacement, in order to exclude aprotinin intrinsic fluorescence or inner filter effects. Fluorescence emission at 340 nm was monitored on a Tecan Safire II instrument (excitation at 280 nm). All experiments were performed in triplicate, and the values were obtained in relative fluorescence units (RFU). Tryptophan quenching was plotted as a curve of the mean and standard deviation of the values against the respective concentrations of the inhibitor in comparison to the fluorescence displacement experiment using Prism 6.01 (Graphpad Software, Inc.).

    Cell Culture

    If not stated otherwise, HeLa, Huh-7, and Vero E6 cells were maintained in DMEM supplemented with 100 U/mL penicillin, 100 μg/mL streptomycin, and 10% heat-inactivated FCS. During infection of Huh-7 cells, DMEM was supplemented with 10 mM HEPES.

    Cloning Strategy

    For construction of the reporter plasmids, the oxygen-dependent degradation domain of hypoxia-inducible factor 1-alpha isoform 1 (HIF1α, corresponding sequence to the amino acids 392 to 715) was cloned from human cDNA into a pRL-sv40 vector backbone (Promega) using Phusion DNA polymerase (NEB). All cloning steps were done by overlap extension PCR. Sequences of primes are found in the Supplementary Information. For amplification of the degradation domain, the primers ODD_fwd and ODD_rev_A/B/C were used. The sequences that were introduced represent the DENV protease NS2A/NS2B cleavage site (ODD_rev_A), the NS3/NS4A cleavage site (ODD_rev_B), and the NS4B/NS5 cleavage (ODD_rev_C). For stable transfection of the reporter plasmid, a puromycin resistance cassette following an EMCV IRES sequence was cloned into the vector downstream of the Renilla reniformis luciferase ORF using the primers RF_IRES-Puro_1 and RF_IRES-Puro_2. As a template for amplification of the protease, DENV genomic DNA was used. The sequence was cloned into the pEF1α-IRES-Neo vector (Addgene plasmid #28019) using the primer pair RF_NS2B3_1 and RF_NS2B3_2.

    Transfection of Mammalian Cells and Stable Cell Line Generation

    All transfections were done using the Lipofectamine 3000 transfection reagent (Life technologies). The plasmid DNA was diluted in Opti-MEM with 0.2 μL of P3000 reagent per 100 ng of DNA and mixed with 0.3 μL of Lipofectamine 3000 transfection reagent per 100 ng of DNA, which was also diluted in Opti-MEM. Ten minutes after mixing, the cell suspension was added and seeded into the respective plates or flasks. For stable transfections, selected antibiotics were added 24 h post-transfection. In brief, 0.8 μg/mL puromycin and 800 μg/mL G418 (Geneticin) were added and the cells were further incubated in DMEM supplemented with 10% FBS for at least 14 days at 37 °C. Afterward, surviving cells were detached, seeded as single cells, and grown out in selected medium in order to generate single-cell clones.

    Measurement of Luminescence (DENV2proHeLa)

    Stable cells were seeded into 96-well plates with a density of 2 × 104 cells per well and treated immediately with inhibitors of DENV protease in a final volume of 100 μL. After incubation for 24 h at 37 °C, the medium was removed and cells were lysed by adding 25 μL lysis buffer (Promega) for 15 min at room temperature. Luciferase activity was recorded using a FLUOstar omega plate reader (BMG labtech) with injections of 100 μL of coelenterazine (2.75 μM in PBS) per well. Luminescence was recorded for 5 s. Measurements for Figure 2B were performed with 100 μL of coelenterazine (1 μM in PBS) per well, and luminescence was recorded for 10 s. Each concentration was assayed in triplicate. Percent inhibition was calculated using an untreated control. For EC50 calculations, data were fitted and calculated with Prism 6.01 (Graphpad Software, Inc.) using a four-parameter nonlinear dose–response curve with background subtraction (wells without cells).

    Virus Titer Reduction Assay

    Huh-7 cells were seeded into 96-well plates at a density of 1 × 104 cells in 50 μL per well and incubated overnight. The next day, cells were infected with DENV serotype 2 for 2 h at an MOI of 1 before medium change and compound addition. Infected cells were then incubated for 48 h in the presence of compound in triplicate wells. In order to determine the EC50, a range of serially diluted compound concentrations starting at 50 μM was used. After 48 h of incubation, supernatants were harvested and triplicates were pooled. These virus-containing supernatants were used to determine the virus titer (reduction) by a plaque assay, as described elsewhere.(60) Briefly, VeroE6 cells were seeded into 24-well plates at a density of 2.5 × 105 cells/well and the next day infected for 1 h with 10-fold serial dilutions of the virus supernatant ranging from 10–1 to 10–6. After medium exchange and addition of the plaque medium, VeroE6 cells were incubated for 7 days. Cells were subsequently fixed with formaldehyde, and plaques were visualized by crystal violet stain. At a suitable dilution, plaques were counted, and the virus titer was calculated and plotted against the respective compound concentration. EC50 values were derived from fitting data to a dose–response curve (variable-slope, nonlinear regression model) using Prism 6.01 (Graphpad Software, Inc.).

    Cytotoxicity

    Cell viability in Huh-7 or Hela cells in the presence of compound dilutions was determined using CellTiter-Blue (Promega) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Plates were prepared in parallel to the cell-based DENV reporter or virus titer reduction assay with analogous treatment. Each concentration was assayed in triplicate.

    Molecular Modeling

    The structures of the ligands were sketched using ChemBioDraw Ultra v.11.0 (CambridgeSoft Corporation, USA), minimized using the MM2 method. The calculations were performed on an Intel(R) Core(TM) 2 Quad CPU Q9450 @ 2.66 GHz running open SuSE 11.0, using GOLD 5.1 and its graphical interface Hermes 1.4.(53) The crystal structure of the NS2B–NS3 protease of DENV serotype 3 in complex with a tetrapeptidyl aldehyde was extracted from the dimer structure 3U1I.(26) All water molecules and sulfate ions were removed, and the structure was further prepared using Dock Prep (Chimera).(56) All hydrogens were added, and the ligand was extracted from the structure. The binding site was defined in a radius of 10 Å around the extracted ligand, and automatic cavity detection was used. A total of 10 different solutions were calculated for every ligand, and the docking results were aligned and visualized using Chimera 11.1.(56)

      The global distribution and burden of dengue
      Bhatt, Samir; Gething, Peter W.; Brady, Oliver J.; Messina, Jane P.; Farlow, Andrew W.; Moyes, Catherine L.; Drake, John M.; Brownstein, John S.; Hoen, Anne G.; Sankoh, Osman; Myers, Monica F.; George, Dylan B.; Jaenisch, Thomas; Wint, G. R. William; Simmons, Cameron P.; Scott, Thomas W.; Farrar, Jeremy J.; Hay, Simon I.
      Nature (London, United Kingdom) (2013), 496 (7446), 504-507CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836. (Nature Publishing Group)
      Dengue is a systemic viral infection transmitted between humans by Aedes mosquitoes. For some patients, dengue is a life-threatening illness. There are currently no licensed vaccines or specific therapeutics, and substantial vector control efforts have not stopped its rapid emergence and global spread. The contemporary worldwide distribution of the risk of dengue virus infection and its public health burden are poorly known. Here we undertake an exhaustive assembly of known records of dengue occurrence worldwide, and use a formal modeling framework to map the global distribution of dengue risk. We then pair the resulting risk map with detailed longitudinal information from dengue cohort studies and population surfaces to infer the public health burden of dengue in 2010. We predict dengue to be ubiquitous throughout the tropics, with local spatial variations in risk influenced strongly by rainfall, temp. and the degree of urbanization. Using cartog. approaches, we est. there to be 390 million (95% credible interval 284-528) dengue infections per yr, of which 96 million (67-136) manifest apparently (any level of clin. or subclin. severity). This infection total is more than three times the dengue burden est. of the World Health Organization. Stratification of our ests. by country allows comparison with national dengue reporting, after taking into account the probability of an apparent infection being formally reported. The most notable differences are discussed. These new risk maps and infection ests. provide novel insights into the global, regional and national public health burden imposed by dengue. We anticipate that they will provide a starting point for a wider discussion about the global impact of this disease and will help to guide improvements in disease control strategies using vaccine, drug and vector control methods, and in their economic evaluation.

      Of cascades and perfect storms: the immunopathogenesis of dengue haemorrhagic fever-dengue shock syndrome (DHF/DSS)
      Pang, Tikki; Cardosa, Mary Jane; Guzman, Maria G.
      Immunology and Cell Biology (2007), 85 (1), 43-45CODEN: ICBIEZ; ISSN:0818-9641. (Nature Publishing Group)
      A review. The past four decades has witnessed a consolidation of the original observations made in the 1970s that dengue haemorrhagic fever (DHF) and dengue shock syndrome (DSS) have an immunol. basis. Following reinfection with a dengue virus of different serotype, severe disease is linked to high levels of antibody-enhanced viral replication early in illness which is followed by a cascade of memory T-cell activation and a 'storm' of inflammatory cytokines and other chem. mediators. These compds. are released mainly from T cells, monocytes/macrophages and endothelial cells, and ultimately cause an increase in vascular permeability. The consolidation of the evidence has been largely due to several important prospective sero-epidemiol. studies in areas endemic for DHF/DSS, which have shown that risk of severe disease is significantly higher in secondary dengue infections. These advances have underscored the fact that DHF/DSS pathogenesis is a complex, multifactorial process involving cocirculation of various dengue virus serotypes and the interplay of host and viral factors that influence disease severity. The continued search to define risk factors in susceptible populations must be combined with the new techniques of mol. virol. and innovative approaches in vaccine design to achieve the ultimate objective of developing a safe and effective vaccine.

      Dengue: a continuing global threat
      Guzman, Maria G.; Halstead, Scott B.; Artsob, Harvey; Buchy, Philippe; Farrar, Jeremy; Gubler, Duane J.; Hunsperger, Elizabeth; Kroeger, Axel; Margolis, Harold S.; Martinez, Eric; Nathan, Michael B.; Pelegrino, Jose Luis; Simmons, Cameron; Yoksan, Sutee; Peeling, Rosanna W.

      Dengue fever and dengue haemorrhagic fever are important arthropod-borne viral diseases. Each year, there are ∼50 million dengue infections and ∼500,000 individuals are hospitalized with dengue haemorrhagic fever, mainly in Southeast Asia, the Pacific and the Americas. Illness is produced by any of the four dengue virus serotypes. A global strategy aimed at increasing the capacity for surveillance and outbreak response, changing behaviors and reducing the disease burden using integrated vector management in conjunction with early and accurate diagnosis has been advocated. Antiviral drugs and vaccines that are currently under development could also make an important contribution to dengue control in the future.

      A review. In 2008, 25 years after the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) was discovered as the then tentative etiol. agent of acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS), exactly 25 anti-HIV compds. have been formally approved for clin. use in the treatment of AIDS.       Structural biology of dengue virus enzymes: Towards rational design of therapeutics
      Noble, Christian G.; Shi, Pei-Yong
      Antiviral Research (2012), 96 (2), 115-126CODEN: ARSRDR; ISSN:0166-3542. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. Development of anti-dengue therapy represents an urgent un-met medical need. Towards antiviral therapy, recent advances in crystal structures of DENV enzymes have led to the possibility of structure-based rational design of inhibitors for anti-dengue therapy. These include (i) the structure of the active' form of the DENV protease in complex with a peptide substrate; (ii) the structure of DENV methyltransferase bound to an inhibitor that selectively suppresses viral methyltransferase, but not human methyltransferases; (iii) the structure of DENV RNA-dependent RNA polymerase in complex with a small-mol. compd. The new structural information has provided new avenues for development of anti-dengue therapy.

      Li, J.; Lim, S. P.; Beer, D.; Patel, V.; Wen, D.; Tumanut, C.; Tully, D. C.; Williams, J. A.; Jiricek, J.; Priestle, J. P.; Harris, J. L.; Vasudevan, S. G. Functional Profiling of Recombinant NS3 Proteases from All Four Serotypes of Dengue Virus Using Tetrapeptide and Octapeptide Substrate Libraries. J. Biol.       12
      Functional Profiling of Recombinant NS3 Proteases from All Four Serotypes of Dengue Virus Using Tetrapeptide and Octapeptide Substrate Libraries
      Li, Jun; Lim, Siew Pheng; Beer, David; Patel, Viral; Wen, Daying; Tumanut, Christine; Tully, David C.; Williams, Jennifer A.; Jiricek, Jan; Priestle, John P.; Harris, Jennifer L.; Vasudevan, Subhash G.
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (2005), 280 (31), 28766-28774CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      Regulated proteolysis by the two-component NS2B/NS3 protease of dengue virus is essential for virus replication and the maturation of infectious virions. The functional similarity between the NS2B/NS3 proteases from the four genetically and antigenically distinct serotypes was addressed by characterizing the differences in their substrate specificity using tetrapeptide and octapeptide libraries in a positional scanning format, each contg. 130,321 substrates. The proteases from different serotypes were shown to be functionally homologous based on the similarity of their substrate cleavage preferences. A strong preference for basic amino acid residues (Arg/Lys) at the P1 positions was obsd., whereas the preferences for the P2-4 sites were in the order of Arg > Thr > Gln/Asn/Lys for P2, Lys > Arg > Asn for P3, and Nle > Leu > Lys > Xaa for P4. The prime site substrate specificity was for small and polar amino acids in P1' and P3'. In contrast, the P2' and P4' substrate positions showed minimal activity. The influence of the P2 and P3 amino acids on ground state binding and the P4 position for transition state stabilization was identified through single substrate kinetics with optimal and suboptimal substrate sequences. The specificities obsd. for dengue NS2B/NS3 have features in common with the physiol. cleavage sites in the dengue polyprotein; however, all sites reveal previously unrecognized suboptimal sequences.
      >> More from SciFinder ®
      https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&resolution=options&coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2MXmvFSnt7w%253D&md5=7e7bd5ab26af480759378090e7e73e66

      Peptide inhibitors of dengue virus NS3 protease. Part 1: Warhead

      Discovery of Nanomolar Dengue and West Nile Virus Protease Inhibitors Containing a 4-Benzyloxyphenylglycine Residue
      Behnam, Mira A. M.; Graf, Dominik; Bartenschlager, Ralf; Zlotos, Darius P.; Klein, Christian D.
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2015), 58 (23), 9354-9370CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      The dengue virus (DENV) and West Nile Virus (WNV) NS2B-NS3 proteases are attractive targets for the development of dual-acting therapeutics against these arboviral pathogens. We present the synthesis and extensive biol. evaluation of inhibitors that contain benzyl ethers of 4-hydroxyphenylglycine as non-natural peptidic building blocks synthesized via a copper-complex intermediate. A three-step optimization strategy, beginning with fragment growth of the C-terminal 4-hydroxyphenylglycine to the benzyloxy ether, followed by C- and N-terminal optimization, and finally fragment merging generated compds. with in vitro affinities in the low nanomolar range. The most promising deriv. reached Ki values of 12 nM at the DENV-2 and 39 nM at the WNV proteases. Several of the newly discovered protease inhibitors yielded a significant redn. of dengue and West Nile virus titers in cell-based assays of virus replication, with an EC50 value of 3.4 μM at DENV-2 and 15.5 μM at WNV for the most active analog.

      Understanding drug-likeness
      Ursu, Oleg; Rayan, Anwar; Goldblum, Amiram; Oprea, Tudor I.
      Wiley Interdisciplinary Reviews: Computational Molecular Science (2011), 1 (5), 760-781CODEN: WIRCAH; ISSN:1759-0884. (Wiley-Blackwell)
      A review. "Drug-likeness", a qual. property of chems. assigned by experts committee vote, is wedely integrated into the early stages of lead and drug discovery. Its conceptual evolution paralleled work related to Pfizer's "rule of five" and lead-likeness, and is placed within this framework. The discrimination between "drugs" (represented by a collection of pharmaceutically relevant small mols., some of which are marketed drugs) and "nondrugs" (typically, chem. reagents) is possible using a wide variety of statistical tools and chem. descriptor systems. Here we summarize 18 papers focused on drug-likeness, and provide a comprehensive overview of progress in the field. Tools that est. drug-likeness are valuable in the early stages of lead discovery, and can be used to filter out compds. with undersirable properties from screening libraries and to prioritize hits from primary screens. As the goal is, most often, to develop orally available drugs, it is also useful to optimize drug-like pharmacokinetic properties. We examine tools that evaluate drug-likeness and some of their shortcomings, challenges facing these tools, and address the following issues: What is the definition of drug-likeness and how can it be utilized to reduce attrition rate in drug discovery. How difficult is it to distinguish drugs from nondrugs. Are nondrug datasets reliable. Can we est. oral drug-likeness. We discuss a drug-like filter and recent advances in the prediction of oral drug-likeness. The heuristic aspect of drug-likeness is also addressed.

      C-Terminal Residue Optimization and Fragment Merging: Discovery of a Potent Peptide-Hybrid Inhibitor of Dengue Protease
      Behnam, Mira A. M.; Nitsche, Christoph; Vechi, Sergio M.; Klein, Christian D.
      ACS Medicinal Chemistry Letters (2014), 5 (9), 1037-1042CODEN: AMCLCT; ISSN:1948-5875. (American Chemical Society)
      Dengue virus protease is a promising target for the development of antiviral drugs. We describe here a two-step rational optimization that led to the discovery of the potent inhibitor 35 with nanomolar binding affinity at dengue protease serotype 2 (IC50 = 0.6 μM, Ki = 0.4 μM). First, a large no. of natural and non-natural amino acids were screened at the C-terminal position of the previously reported, canonical peptide sequence (Cap-Arg-Lys-Nle-NH2). Compared to the ref. compd. 1 (Bz-Arg-Lys-Nle-NH2, IC50 = 13.3 μM), a 4-fold higher inhibitory potential was obsd. with the incorporation of a C-terminal phenylglycine (compd. 9, IC50 = 3.3 μM). Second, we applied fragment merging of 9 with the previously reported thiazolidinedione peptide hybrid 33 (IC50 = 2.5 μM). This approach led to the fusion of two inhibitor-fragments with micromolar affinity into a 20-fold more potent, competitive inhibitor of dengue protease.

      Kuiper, Benjamin D.; Slater, Kristin; Spellmon, Nicholas; Holcomb, Joshua; Medapureddy, Prasanna; Muzzarelli, Kendall M.; Yang, Zhe; Ovadia, Reuben; Amblard, Franck; Kovari, Iulia A.; Schinazi, Raymond F.; Kovari, Ladislau C.

      Zika virus (ZIKV) is a flavivirus spread by daytime-active Aedes spp. mosquitoes such as A. aegypti and A. albopictus. Previously thought to be a mild infection, the latest ZIKV outbreak in the Americas is causally assocd. with more severe symptoms as well as severe birth defects, such as microcephaly.       Ligand-bound structures of the dengue virus protease reveal the active conformation
      Noble, Christian G.; Seh, Cheah Chen; Chao, Alexander T.; Shi, Pei Yong
      Journal of Virology (2012), 86 (1), 438-446CODEN: JOVIAM; ISSN:0022-538X. (American Society for Microbiology)
      Dengue is a mosquito-borne viral hemorrhagic disease that is a major threat to human health in tropical and subtropical regions. Here we report crystal structures of a peptide covalently bound to dengue virus serotype 3 (DENV-3) protease as well as the serine-protease inhibitor aprotinin bound to the same enzyme. These structures reveal, for the first time, a catalytically active, closed conformation of the DENV protease. In the presence of the peptide, the DENV-3 protease forms the closed conformation in which the hydrophilic β-hairpin region of NS2B wraps around the NS3 protease core, in a manner analogous to the structure of West Nile virus (WNV) protease. Our results confirm that flavivirus proteases form the closed conformation during proteolysis, as previously proposed for WNV. The current DENV-3 protease structures reveal the detailed interactions at the P4' to P3 sites of the substrate. The new structural information explains the sequence preference, particularly for long basic residues in the nonprime side, as well as the difference in substrate specificity between the WNV and DENV proteases at the prime side. Structural anal. of the DENV-3 protease-peptide complex revealed a pocket that is formed by residues from NS2B and NS3; this pocket also exists in the WNV NS2B/NS3 protease structure and could be targeted for potential antivirus development. The structural information presented in the current study is invaluable for the design of specific inhibitors of DENV protease.

      Generation of destabilized green fluorescent protein as a transcription reporter
      Li, Xianqiang; Zhao, Xiaoning; Fang, Yu; Jiang, Xin; Duong, Tommy; Fan, Connie; Huang, Chiao-Chain; Kain, Steven R.
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (1998), 273 (52), 34970-34975CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      The green fluorescent protein (GFP) is a widely used reporter in gene expression and protein localization studies. GFP is a stable protein; this property allows its accumulation and easy detection in cells. However, this stability also limits its application in studies that require rapid reporter turnover. We created a destabilized GFP for use in such studies by fusing amino acids 422-461 of the degrdn. domain of mouse ornithine decarboxylase (MODC) to the C-terminal end of an enhanced variant of GFP (EGFP). The fusion protein, unlike EGFP, was unstable in the presence of cycloheximide and had a fluorescence half-life of 2 h. Western blot anal. indicated that the fluorescence decay of EGFP-MODC-(422-461) was correlated with degrdn. of the fusion protein. We mutated key amino acids in the PEST sequence of EGFP-MODC-(422-461) and identified several mutants with variable half-lives. The suitability of destabilized EGFP as a transcription reporter was tested by linking it to NFκB binding sequences and monitoring tumor necrosis factor α-mediated NFκB activation. We obtained time course induction and dose response kinetics similar to secreted alk. phosphatase obtained in transfected cells. This result did not occur when unmodified EGFP was used as the reporter. Because of its autofluorescence, destabilized EGFP can be used to directly correlate gene induction with biochem. change, such as NFκB translocation to the nucleus.

      Regulation of hypoxia-inducible factor 1α is mediated by an O2-dependent degradation domain via the ubiquitin-proteasome pathway
      Huang, L. Eric; Gu, Jie; Schau, Maureen; Bunn, H. Franklin
      Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America (1998), 95 (14), 7987-7992CODEN: PNASA6; ISSN:0027-8424. (National Academy of Sciences)
      Hypoxia induces a group of physiol. important genes such as erythropoietin and vascular endothelial growth factor. These genes are transcriptionally up-regulated by hypoxia-inducible factor 1 (HIF-1), a global regulator that belongs to the basic helix-loop-helix PAS family. Although HIF-1 is a heterodimer composed of α and β subunits, its activity is primarily detd. by hypoxia-induced stabilization of HIF-1α which is otherwise rapidly degraded in oxygenated cells. The authors report the identification of an oxygen-dependent degrdn. (ODD) domain within HIF-1α that controls its degrdn. by the ubiquitin-proteasome pathway. The ODD domain consists of ≈200 amino acid residues, located in the central region of HIF-1α. Because portions of the domain independently confer degrdn. of HIF-1α, deletion of this entire region is required to give rise to a stable HIF-1α, capable of heterodimerization, DNA-binding, and transactivation in the absence of hypoxic signaling. Conversely, the ODD domain alone confers oxygen-dependent instability when fused to a stable protein, Gal4. Hence, the ODD domain plays a pivotal role for regulating HIF-1 activity and thereby may provide a means of controlling gene expression by changes in oxygen tension.

      Hypoxia-inducible factor (HIF-1)α: its protein stability and biological functions
      Lee, Ji-Won; Bae, Seong-Hui; Jeong, Joo-Won; Kim, Se-Hee; Kim, Kyu-Won
      Experimental and Molecular Medicine (2004), 36 (1), 1-12CODEN: EMMEF3; ISSN:1226-3613. (Korean Society of Medical Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      A review. Hypoxia-inducible factor (HIF-1) is an oxygen-dependent transcriptional activator, which plays crucial roles in the angiogenesis of tumors and mammalian development. HIF-1 consists of a constitutively expressed HIF-1β subunit and one of three subunits (HIF-1α, HIF-2α or HIF-3α). The stability and activity of HIF-1α are regulated by various post-translational modifications, hydroxylation, acetylation, and phosphorylation. Therefore, HIF-1α interacts with several protein factors including PHD, pVHL, ARD-1, and p300/CBP. Under normoxia, the HIF-1α subunit is rapidly degraded via the von Hippel-Lindau tumor suppressor gene product (pVHL)-mediated ubiquitin-proteasome pathway. The assocn. of pVHL and HIF-1α under normoxic conditions is triggered by the hydroxylation of prolines and the acetylation of lysine within a polypeptide segment known as the oxygen-dependent degrdn. (ODD) domain. On the contrary, in the hypoxia condition, HIF-1α subunit becomes stable and interacts with coactivators such as p300/CBP to modulate its transcriptional activity. Eventually, HIF-1 acts as a master regulator of numerous hypoxia-inducible genes under hypoxic conditions. The target genes of HIF-1 are esp. related to angiogenesis, cell proliferation/survival, and glucose/iron metab. Moreover, it was reported that the activation of HIF-1α is closely assocd. with a variety of tumors and oncogenic pathways. Hence, the blocking of HIF-1α itself or HIF-1α interacting proteins inhibit tumor growth. Based on these findings, HIF-1 can be a prime target for anticancer therapies. This review summarizes the mol. mechanism of HIF-1α stability, the bi- ol. functions of HIF-1 and its potential applications of cancer therapies.

      Independent function of two destruction domains in hypoxia-inducible factor-α chains activated by prolyl hydroxylation
      Masson, Norma; Willam, Carsten; Maxwell, Patrick H.; Pugh, Christopher W.; Ratcliffe, Peter J.
      EMBO Journal (2001), 20 (18), 5197-5206CODEN: EMJODG; ISSN:0261-4189. (Oxford University Press)
      Oxygen-dependent proteolytic destruction of hypoxia-inducible factor-α (HIF-α) subunits plays a central role in regulating transcriptional responses to hypoxia. Recent studies have defined a key function for the von Hippel-Lindau tumor suppressor E3 ubiquitin ligase (VHLE3) in this process, and have defined an interaction with HIF-1α that is regulated by prolyl hydroxylation. Here we show that two independent regions within the HIF-α oxygen-dependent degrdn. domain (ODDD) are targeted for ubiquitylation by VHLE3 in a manner dependent upon prolyl hydroxylation. The definition of two independent destruction domains implicates a more complex system of pVHL-HIF-α interactions, but reinforces the role of prolyl hydroxylation as an oxygen-dependent destruction signal.

      In vivo pharmacodynamic imaging of proteasome inhibition
      Kimbrel, Erin A.; Davis, Tina N.; Bradner, James E.; Kung, Andrew L.
      Molecular Imaging (2009), 8 (3), 140-147CODEN: MIOMBP; ISSN:1535-3508. (BC Decker Inc.)
      Inhibiting the proteolytic activity of the 26S proteasome has been shown to have selective apoptotic effects on cancer cells and to be clin. efficacious in certain malignancies. There is an unmet medical need for addnl. proteasome inhibitors, and their development will be facilitated by surrogate markers of proteasome function. Toward this end, ectopic fusion of the destruction domain from ornithine decarboxylase (ODC) to reporter proteins is often used for assessing proteasome function. For luciferase-based reporters, we hypothesized that the oxygen-dependent destruction domain (ODD) from hypoxia-inducible factor 1α (HIF-1α) may provide improved sensitivity over luciferase-ODC, owing to its extremely rapid turnover by the proteasome (HIF-1α has a half-life of less than 5 min). Importantly, ODD-luciferase also serves as an effective in vivo marker of proteasome function in xenograft tumor models, with inhibition being detected by noninvasive imaging within 3 h of bortezomib administration. These data establish ODD-luciferase as a surrogate marker of proteasome function that can be used both in vitro and in vivo for the development of novel proteasome inhibitors.

      Use of the ODD-luciferase transgene for the non-invasive imaging of spontaneous tumors in mice
      Goldman, Scott J.; Chen, Elizabeth; Taylor, Robert; Zhang, Sheng; Petrosky, Whitney; Reiss, Michael; Jin, Shengkan
      PLoS One (2011), 6 (3), e18269CODEN: POLNCL; ISSN:1932-6203. (Public Library of Science)

      Functional Characterization of cis and trans Activity of the Flavivirus NS2B-NS3 Protease
      Bera, Aloke K.; Kuhn, Richard J.; Smith, Janet L.
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (2007), 282 (17), 12883-12892CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      Flaviviruses are serious human pathogens for which treatments are generally lacking. The proteolytic maturation of the 375-kDa viral polyprotein is one target for antiviral development. The flavivirus serine protease consists of the N-terminal domain of the multifunctional nonstructural protein 3 (NS3) and an essential 40-residue cofactor (NS2B40) within viral protein NS2B. The NS2B-NS3 protease is responsible for all cytoplasmic cleavage events in viral polyprotein maturation. This study describes the first biochem. characterization of flavivirus protease activity using full-length NS3. Recombinant proteases were created by fusion of West Nile virus (WNV) NS2B40 to full-length WNV NS3. The protease catalyzed two autolytic cleavages. The NS2B/NS3 junction was cleaved before protein purifn. A second site at Arg459↓Gly460 within the C-terminal helicase region of NS3 was cleaved more slowly. Autolytic cleavage reactions also occurred in NS2B-NS3 recombinant proteins from yellow fever virus, dengue virus types 2 and 4, and Japanese encephalitis virus. Cis and trans cleavages were distinguished using a noncleavable WNV protease variant and two types of substrates as follows: an inactive variant of recombinant WNV NS2B-NS3, and cyan and yellow fluorescent proteins fused by a dodecamer peptide encompassing a natural cleavage site. With these materials, the autolytic cleavages were found to be intramol. only. Autolytic cleavage of the helicase site was insensitive to protein diln., confirming that autolysis is intramol. Formation of an active protease was found to require neither cleavage of NS2B from NS3 nor a free NS3 N terminus. Evidence was also obtained for product inhibition of the protease by the cleaved C terminus of NS2B.

      Dengue virus (DENV) causes disease globally, with an estd. 25 to 100 million new infections per yr. At present, no effective vaccine is available, and treatment is supportive. In this study, we identified BP2109, a potent and selective small-mol. inhibitor of the DENV NS2B/NS3 protease, by a high-throughput screening assay using a recombinant protease complex consisting of the central hydrophilic portion of NS2B and the N terminus of the protease domain. BP2109 inhibited DENV (serotypes 1 to 4), but not Japanese encephalitis virus (JEV), replication and viral RNA synthesis without detectable cytotoxicity. The compd. inhibited recombinant DENV-2 NS2B/NS3 protease with a 50% inhibitory concn. (IC50) of 15.43 ± 2.12 μM and reduced the reporter expression of the DENV-2 replicon with a 50% effective concn. (EC50) of 0.17 ± 0.01 μM. Sequencing analyses of several individual clones derived from BP2109-resistant DENV-2 RNAs revealed that two amino acid substitutions (R55K and E80K) are found in the region of NS2B, a cofactor of the NS2B/NS3 protease complex. The introduction of R55K and E80K double mutations into the dengue virus NS2B/NS3 protease and a dengue virus replicon construct conferred 10.3- and 73.8-fold resistance to BP2109, resp. The E80K mutation was further detd. to be the key mutation conferring dengue virus replicon resistance (61.3-fold) to BP2109, whereas the R55K mutation alone did not affect resistance to BP2109. Both the R55K and E80K mutations are located in the central hydrophilic portion of the NS2B cofactor, where extensive interactions with the NS3pro domain exist. Thus, our data provide evidence that BP2109 likely inhibits DENV by a novel mechanism.
